at &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as396982\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS396982 (GOOGLE-CLOUD-PLATFORM)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, in what appears to be a full port scan across all IP address ranges. (The ZMap client responsibly self-identifies, based on ZMapâs responsible self-identification as discussed later.) We see a similar level of scan traffic from IP prefixes in AS396982 geolocated to the United States.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/756bijZU4xJpMvc78ZdbYl/370d1b939bf2023bb2fee550df7b492c/1622-15.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-15\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/AS396982?dateStart=2024-07-28&amp;dateEnd=2024-08-26#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;zero-rating-in-mobile-networks\&quot;&gt;Zero-rating in mobile networks&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#zero-rating-in-mobile-networks\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;A cursory look at anomaly rates at the country level reveals some interesting findings. For instance, looking at connections from &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/mx\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Mexico&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, the rates of Post-ACK and Post-PSH anomalies often associated with connection tampering are higher than the global average. The profile for Mexico connections is also similar to others in the region. However, Mexico is a country with â&lt;a href=\&quot;https://freedomhouse.org/country/mexico/freedom-net/2023\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;no documented evidence that the government or other actors block or filter internet content.&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;â&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5bZjzlkqtRzmB5097m6z6S/e2e07a884a510d963b97c5e6ffd00ee2/1622-16.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-16\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/mx?dateStart=2024-07-28&amp;dateEnd=2024-08-26#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Looking at each of the top ASes by HTTP traffic volume in Mexico, we find that close to 50% of connections from &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as28403\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS28403 (RadioMovil Dipsa, S.A. de C.V., operating as Telcel)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; are terminated via a reset or timeout directly after the completion of the TCP handshake (Post-ACK connection stage). In this stage, itâs possible a middlebox has seen and dropped a data packet before it gets to Cloudflare.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One explanation for this behavior may be &lt;a href=\&quot;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zero-rating\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;zero-rating&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, in which a cellular network provider allows access to certain resources (such as messaging or social media apps) at no cost. When users exceed their data transfer limits on their account, the provider might still allow traffic to zero-rated destinations while blocking connections to other resources.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To enforce a zero-rating policy, an ISP might use the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/learning/ssl/what-is-sni/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;TLS Server Name Indication (SNI)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to determine whether to block or allow connections. The SNI is sent in a data-containing packet immediately following the TCP handshake. Thus, if an ISP drops the packet containing the SNI, the server would still see the SYN and ACK packets from the client but no subsequent packets, which is consistent with a Post-ACK connection anomaly.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7KUfARxvDEogTFeh7PL4n5/68dc237c07a60e431cc2d2654cade2f7/1622-17.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-17\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/AS28403?dateStart=2024-07-28&amp;dateEnd=2024-08-26#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Turning to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/pe\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Peru&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, another country with a similar profile in the dataset, there are even higher rates of Post-ACK and Post-PSH anomalies compared to Mexico.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/1vLO0sP2Ap3206PX4Ysdim/a4f0274b4d884703ff4b4d879a667d90/1622-18.png\&quot; shows high rates of Post-ACK anomalies similar to AS28403 in Mexico. Both networks are operated by the same parent company, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.americamovil.com/English/overview/default.aspx\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AmÃ©rica MÃ³vil&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, so one might expect similar network policies and network management techniques to be employed.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/1doJKHsOHyYlY6t3LLVmdm/147d8239981bc580b9fd53cf2b7b4d89/1622-19.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-19\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/AS12252?dateStart=2024-07-28&amp;dateEnd=2024-08-26#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Interestingly, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as6147\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS6147 (TelefÃ³nica Del PerÃº)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; instead shows high rates of Post-PSH connection anomalies. This could indicate that this network uses different techniques at the network layer to enforce its policies.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/44i6wdU8oWl8CTl1YDZVES/7ac4728c15e4a0627f262f8dcd791be2/1622-20.png\&quot; At that time we were preparing the TCP resets and timeouts dataset, which was helpful to confirm outside reports and get some insight into the specific techniques used for the shutdowns.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As examples of changing behavior, we can go âback in timeâ to observe exam-related blocking as it happened. In &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/sy\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Syria&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, during the exam-related shutdowns we see spikes in the rate of Post-SYN anomalies. In reality, we see a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic/sy?dateStart=2024-05-20&amp;dateEnd=2024-06-19\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;near-total drop in traffic&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (including SYN packets) during these periods.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3K5o8qI2wzozKrFlSm9x9S/d7778ba433055988ead0726ede69423c/1622-21.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-21\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/sy?dateStart=2024-05-20&amp;dateEnd=2024-06-19#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\nA &lt;a href=\&quot;https://x.com/CloudflareRadar/status/1816438072768078078\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;second round&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; of shutdowns starting the last week of July are quite prominent as well.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3YJVpto9QJBCkDm5DVLiVN/23a4b168065ebf47a062f89fc98d5048/1622-22.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-21\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/sy?dateStart=2024-07-20&amp;dateEnd=2024-08-19#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Looking at connections from &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/iq\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Iraq&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Cloudflareâs view of exam-related shutdowns appears similar to those in Syria, with multiple Post-SYN spikes, albeit much less pronounced.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/2KZyGLIOyoEFH3sYTiuHzK/5539aba3877760e90bacf6889e51e0ad/1622-23.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-23\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/iq?dateStart=2024-05-17&amp;dateEnd=2024-06-10#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/syria-iraq-algeria-exam-internet-shutdown\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;exams shutdown blog&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; also describes how &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/dz\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Algeria&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; took a more nuanced approach for restricting access to content during exam times: instead of full Internet shutdowns, evidence suggests that Algeria instead targeted specific connections. Indeed, during exam periods we see an increase in Post-ACK connection anomalies. This behavior would be expected if a middlebox selectively drops packets that contain forbidden content, while leaving other packets alone (like the initial SYN and ACK).&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card \&quot;&gt;\n            \n            &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/67LrJ8cb1XpYsEpzENs54M/775ee5671ef738a0afd93fc0a55b289a/1622-24.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;1622-24\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n            \n            &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;via &lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks/dz?dateStart=2024-06-06&amp;dateEnd=2024-06-19#tcp-resets-and-attacks\&quot;&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;i&gt;Cloudflare Radar&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The examples above reinforce that this data is most useful when correlated with other signals. The data is also available via the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/operations/radar-get-tcp-resets-timeouts-summary\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, so others can dive in more deeply. Our detection techniques are also transferable to other servers and operators, as described next.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;h2 id=\&quot;anomalousTCP\&quot;&gt;How to detect anomalous TCP connections at scale&lt;/h2&gt;&lt;p&gt;In this section, we discuss how we constructed the TCP resets and timeouts dataset. The scale of Cloudflareâs global network presents unique challenges for data processing and analysis. We share our techniques to help readers to understand our methodology, interpret the dataset, and replicate the mechanisms in other networks or servers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our methodology can be summarized as follows:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Log a sample of connections arriving at our client-facing servers. This sampling system is completely passive, meaning that it has no ability to decrypt traffic and only has access to existing packets sent over the network.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Reconstruct connections from the captured packets. A novel aspect of our design is that only one direction needs to be observed, from client to server.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Match reconstructed connections against a set of signatures for anomalous connections terminated by resets or timeouts. These signatures consist of two parts: a &lt;i&gt;connection stage&lt;/i&gt;, and a set of &lt;i&gt;tags&lt;/i&gt; that indicate specific behaviors derived from the literature and our own observations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;&lt;p&gt;These design choices keep encrypted packets safe and can be replicated anywhere, without needing access to the destination server.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;first-sample-connections\&quot;&gt;First, sample connections&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#first-sample-connections\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Our main goal was to design a mechanism that scales, and gives us broad visibility into all connections arriving at Cloudflareâs network. Running traffic captures on each client-facing server works, but does not scale. We would also need to know exactly where and when to look, making continuous insights hard to capture. Instead, we sample connections from all of Cloudflareâs servers and log them to a central location where we could perform offline analysis.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is where we hit the first roadblock: existing packet logging pipelines used by Cloudflareâs analytics systems log individual packets, but a connection consists of many packets. To detect connection anomalies we needed to see all, or at least enough, packets in a given connection. Fortunately, we were able to leverage a flexible logging system built by Cloudflareâs DoS team for &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/ddos-protection/about/how-ddos-protection-works/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;analyzing packets involved in DDoS attacks&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; in conjunction with a carefully crafted invocation of two &lt;a href=\&quot;https://manpages.debian.org/bookworm/iptables/iptables.8.en.html\&quot;&gt;&lt;code&gt;&lt;u&gt;iptables&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/a&gt; rules to achieve our goal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The first &lt;code&gt;iptables&lt;/code&gt; rule randomly selects and marks new connections for sampling. In our case, we settled on sampling one in every 10,000 ingress TCP connections. Thereâs nothing magical about this number, but at Cloudflareâs scale it strikes a balance between capturing enough without straining our data processing and analytics pipelines. The &lt;code&gt;iptables&lt;/code&gt; rules only apply to packets after they have passed the DDoS mitigation system. As TCP connections can be long-lived, we sample only new TCP connections. Here is the &lt;code&gt;iptables&lt;/code&gt; rule for marking connections to be sampled:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-bash\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-bash\&quot;&gt;-t mangle -A PREROUTING -p tcp --syn -m state \n--state NEW -m statistic --mode random \n--probability 0.0001 -m connlabel --label &amp;lt;label&amp;gt; \n--set -m comment --comment &amp;quot;Label a sample of ingress TCP connections&amp;quot;\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Breaking this down, the rule is installed in the &lt;code&gt;mangle&lt;/code&gt; table (for modifying packets) in the chain that handles incoming packets (&lt;code&gt;-A PREROUTING&lt;/code&gt;). Only TCP packets with the SYN flag set are considered (&lt;code&gt;-p tcp --syn&lt;/code&gt;) where there is no prior state for the connection (&lt;code&gt;--state NEW&lt;/code&gt;). The filter selects one in every 10,000 SYN packets (&lt;code&gt;-m statistic âmode random --probability 0.0001&lt;/code&gt;) and applies a label to the connection (&lt;code&gt;-m connlabel --label &amp;lt;label&amp;gt; --set&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The second iptables rule logs subsequent packets in the connection, to a maximum of 10 packets. Again, thereâs nothing magic about the number 10 other than that itâs generally enough to capture the connection establishment, subsequent request packets, and resets on connections that close before expected.&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-bash\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-bash\&quot;&gt;-t mangle -A PREROUTING -m connlabel --label \n&amp;lt;label&amp;gt; -m connbytes ! --connbytes 11 \n--connbytes-dir original --connbytes-mode packets \n-j NFLOG --nflog-prefix &amp;quot;&amp;lt;logging flags&amp;gt;&amp;quot; -m \ncomment --comment &amp;quot;Log the first 10 incoming packets of each sampled ingress connection&amp;quot;\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This rule is installed in the same chain as the previous rule. It matches only packets from sampled connections &lt;code&gt;(-m connlabel --label &amp;lt;label&amp;gt;)&lt;/code&gt;, and only the first 10 packets from each connection (&lt;code&gt;-m connbytes ! --connbytes 11 --connbytes-dir original --connbytes-mode packets&lt;/code&gt;). Matched packets are sent to NFLOG (&lt;code&gt;-j NFLOG --nflog-prefix &amp;quot;&amp;lt;logging flags&amp;gt;&amp;quot;&lt;/code&gt;) where theyâre picked up by the logging system and saved to a centralized location for offline analysis.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;reconstructing-connections-from-sampled-packets\&quot;&gt;Reconstructing connections from sampled packets&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#reconstructing-connections-from-sampled-packets\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Packets logged on our servers are inserted into &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/tag/clickhouse/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;ClickHouse&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; tables as part of our analytics pipeline. Each logged packet is stored in its own row in the database. The next challenge is to reassemble packets into the corresponding connections for further analysis. Before we go further, we need to define what a âconnectionâ is for the purpose of this analysis.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We use the standard definition of a connection defined by the network 5-tuple of &lt;code&gt;protocol&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;source IP address&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;source port&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;destination IP address&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;destination port&lt;/code&gt; with the following tweaks:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;We only sample packets on the &lt;i&gt;ingress&lt;/i&gt; (client-to-server) half of a connection, so do not see the corresponding response packets from server to client. In most cases, we can infer what the server response will be based on our knowledge of how our servers are configured. Ultimately, the ingress packets are sufficient to learn anomalous TCP connection behaviors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;We query the ClickHouse dataset in 15-minute intervals, and group together packets sharing the same network 5-tuple within that interval. This means that connections may be truncated towards the end of the query interval. When analyzing connection timeouts, we exclude incomplete flows where the latest packet timestamp is within 10 seconds of the query cutoff.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since resets and timeouts are most likely to affect &lt;i&gt;new&lt;/i&gt; connections, we only consider sequences of packets starting with a SYN packet marking the beginning of a new TCP handshake. Thus, existing long-lived connections are excluded.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;The logging system does not guarantee precise packet interarrival timestamps, so we consider only the set of packets that arrive, not ordered by their arrival time. In some cases, we can determine packet ordering based on TCP sequence numbers but it turns out not to significantly impact the results.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;We filter out a small fraction of connections with multiple SYN packets to reduce noise in the analysis.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;With the above conditions for how we define a connection, weâre now ready to describe our analysis pipeline in more detail.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;mapping-connection-close-events-to-stages\&quot;&gt;Mapping connection close events to stages&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#mapping-connection-close-events-to-stages\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;TCP connections transition through a series of stages from connection establishment through eventual close. The stage at which an anomalous connection closes provides clues as to &lt;i&gt;why&lt;/i&gt; the anomaly occurred. Based on the packets that we receive at our servers, we place each incoming connection into one of four stages (Post-SYN, Post-ACK, Post-PSH, Later), described in more detail &lt;a href=\&quot;#dataset\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;above&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The connection close stage alone provides useful insights into anomalous TCP connections from various networks, and this is what is shown today on Cloudflare Radar. However, in some cases we can provide deeper insights by matching connections against more specific signatures.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;applying-tags-to-describe-more-specific-connection-behaviors\&quot;&gt;Applying tags to describe more specific connection behaviors&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#applying-tags-to-describe-more-specific-connection-behaviors\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;The grouping of connections into stages as described above is done solely based on the TCP flags of packets in the connection. Considering other factors such as packet inter-arrival timing, exact combinations of TCP flags, and other packet fields (IP identification, IP TTL, TCP sequence and acknowledgement numbers, TCP window size, etc.) can allow for more fine-grained matching to specific behaviors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, the popular &lt;a href=\&quot;http://zmap.io\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;ZMap&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; scanner software fixes the IP identification field to 54321 and the TCP window size to 65535 in SYN packets that it generates (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/zmap/zmap/blob/c88db2917612328c843561101495e5263ef7ac5b/src/probe_modules/packet.c\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;source code&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). When we see packets arriving to our network that have these exact fields set, it is likely that the packet was generated by a scanner using ZMap.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Tags can also be used to match connections against known signatures of tampering middleboxes. A large body of active measurements work (for instance, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.ndss-symposium.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/weav.pdf\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Weaver, Sommer, and Paxson&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) has found that some middleboxes deployments exhibit consistent behaviors when disrupting connections via reset injection, such as setting an IP TTL field that differs from other packets sent by the client, or sending both a RST packet and a RST+ACK packet. For more details on specific connection tampering signatures, see the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/connection-tampering\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;blog post&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://research.cloudflare.com/publications/SundaraRaman2023/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;peer-reviewed paper&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Currently, we define the following tags, which we intend to refine and expand over time. Some tags only apply if another tag is also set, as indicated by the hierarchical presentation below (e.g., the &lt;code&gt;fin&lt;/code&gt; tag can only apply when the &lt;code&gt;reset&lt;/code&gt; tag is also set).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;timeout&lt;/code&gt;: terminated due to a timeout&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;reset&lt;/code&gt;: terminated due to a reset (packet with RST flag set)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;fin: at least one FIN packet was received alongside one or more RST packets&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;single_rst&lt;/code&gt;: terminated with a single RST packet&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;multiple_rsts&lt;/code&gt;: terminated with multiple RST packets&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;acknumsame&lt;/code&gt;: the acknowledgement numbers in the RST packets were all the same and non-zero&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;acknumsame0&lt;/code&gt;: the acknowledgement numbers in the RST packets were all zero&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;acknumdif&lt;/code&gt;f: the acknowledgement numbers in the RST packets were different and all non-zero&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;acknumdiff0&lt;/code&gt;: the acknowledgement numbers in the RST packets were different and one was zero&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;single_rstack&lt;/code&gt;: terminated with a single RST+ACK packet (both RST and ACK flags set)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;multiple_rstacks&lt;/code&gt;: terminated with a multiple RST+ACK packets&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;rst_and_rstacks&lt;/code&gt;: terminated with a combination of RST and RST+ACK packets&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;zmap&lt;/code&gt;: SYN packet matches those generated by the ZMap scanner&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;Connection tags are not currently visible in the Radar &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;dashboard&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/operations/radar-get-tcp-resets-timeouts-summary\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, but we plan to release this additional functionality in the future.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h2 id=\&quot;whats-next\&quot;&gt;Whatâs next?&lt;/h2&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#whats-next\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n          &lt;p&gt;Cloudflareâs mission is to help build a better Internet, and we consider transparency and accountability to be a critical part of that mission. We hope that the insights and tools we are sharing help to shed light on anomalous network behaviors around the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While the current TCP resets and timeouts dataset should immediately prove useful to network operators, researchers, and Internet citizens as a whole, weâre not stopping here. There are several improvements weâd like to add in the future:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Expand the set of tags for capturing specific network behaviors and expose them in the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/operations/radar-get-tcp-resets-timeouts-summary\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;dashboard&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Extend insights to connections from Cloudflare to customer origin servers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Add support for QUIC, which is currently used for over &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/adoption-and-usage\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;30% of HTTP requests&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to Cloudflare worldwide.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;If youâve found this blog interesting, we encourage you to read the accompanying &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/connection-tampering\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;blog post&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://research.cloudflare.com/publications/SundaraRaman2023/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;paper&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; for a deep dive on connection tampering, and to explore the TCP resets and timeouts &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security-and-attacks#tcp-resets-and-timeouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;dashboard&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/operations/radar-get-tcp-resets-timeouts-summary\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; on Cloudflare Radar. We welcome you to reach out to us with your own questions and observations at &lt;a Rocket Alertsâ application targets Israeli phone calls, SMS, and user information</h1><p class="f3 fw5 gray5 db di-l mt2">2023-10-14</p><ul class="author-lists flex pl0 mt4"><li class="list flex items-center pr2 mb1-ns"><a href="/author/blake" class="static-avatar pr1"><img class="author-profile-image br-100 mr2" src="https://blog.cloudflare.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto,dpr=3,width=64,height=64,gravity=face,fit=crop,zoom=0.5/https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/4bZNxcx9X9SxXvCtiKTmoX/b155cbc0977be2f3ba5a443f19fb692a/blake.jpeg" alt="Blake DarchÃ©" width="62" height="62"/></a><div class="author-name-tooltip"><a href="/author/blake" class="fw4 f3 no-underline black mr3">Blake DarchÃ©</a></div></li><li class="list flex items-center pr2 mb1-ns"><a href="/author/armen" class="static-avatar pr1"><img class="author-profile-image br-100 mr2" src="https://blog.cloudflare.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto,dpr=3,width=64,height=64,gravity=face,fit=crop,zoom=0.5/https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5FUxL3fp1ZFpPvHTop1LUI/0b3c4e838b2a6eecf79d6ffc27a22f7f/armen.png" alt="Armen Boursalian" width="62" height="62"/></a><div class="author-name-tooltip"><a href="/author/armen" class="fw4 f3 no-underline black mr3">Armen Boursalian</a></div></li><li class="list flex items-center pr2 mb1-ns"><a href="/author/javier" class="static-avatar pr1"><img class="author-profile-image br-100 mr2" src="https://blog.cloudflare.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto,dpr=3,width=64,height=64,gravity=face,fit=crop,zoom=0.5/https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3hJsvxP0uRGmk4DjS9IdSW/0197c661fe20e1ebc9922768d727df02/javier.png" alt="Javier Castro" width="62" height="62"/></a><div class="author-name-tooltip"><a href="/author/javier" class="fw4 f3 no-underline black mr3">Javier Castro</a></div></li></ul><section class="post-full-content"><div class="mb2 gray5">4 min read</div><div class="post-content lh-copy gray1">
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/16uIyWaGcO2XWUU8LFgfIF/951821609d1e44805c99e051818bc857/pasted-image-0--15-.png" alt="Malicious âRedAlert - Rocket Alertsâ application targets Israeli phone calls, SMS, and user information" class="kg-image" width="1600" height="895" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p>On October 13, 2023, Cloudflareâs Cloudforce One Threat Operations Team became aware of a website hosting a Google Android Application (APK) impersonating the legitimate RedAlert - Rocket Alerts application (<a href="https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.red.alert&hl=en&pli=1">https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.red.alert&amp;hl=en&amp;pli=1</a>). Â More than 5,000 rockets have been launched into Israel since the attacks from Hamas began on October 7th 2023. Â RedAlert - Rocket Alerts developed by Elad Nava allows individuals to receive timely and precise alerts about incoming airstrikes. Many people living in Israel rely on these alerts to seek safety - a service which has become increasingly important given the newest escalations in the region.</p><p>Applications alerting of incoming airstrikes have become targets as only days ago, Pro-Palestinian hacktivist group AnonGhost exploited a vulnerability in another application, âRed Alert: Israelâ by Kobi Snir. (<a href="https://cybernews.com/cyber-war/israel-redalert-breached-anonghost-hamas/">https://cybernews.com/cyber-war/israel-redalert-breached-anonghost-hamas/</a>) Their exploit allowed them to intercept requests, expose servers and APIs, and send fake alerts to some app users, including a message that a ânuclear bomb is comingâ. AnonGhost also claimed they attacked other rocket alert applications, including RedAlert by Elad Nava. As of October 11, 2023, the RedAlert app was reportedly functioning normally.</p><p>In the last two days, a new malicious website (<i>hxxps://redalerts[.]me</i>) has advertised the download of well-known open source application RedAlert by Elad Nava (<a href="https://github.com/eladnava/redalert-android">https://github.com/eladnava/redalert-android</a>). Domain impersonation continues to be a popular vector for attackers, as the legitimate website for the application (<i>hxxps://redalert[.]me</i> ) differs from the malicious website by only one letter. Further, threat actors continue to exploit open source code and deploy modified, malicious versions to unsuspecting users.</p><p>The malicious website hosted links to both the iOS and the Android version of the RedAlert app. But while the link to the Apple App Store referred to the legitimate version of the RedAlert app by Elad Nava, the link supposedly referring to the Android version hosted on the Play Store directly downloads a malicious APK file. This attack demonstrates the danger of sideloading applications directly from the Internet as opposed to installing applications from the approved app store.</p><p>The malicious RedAlert version imitates the legitimate rocket alert application but simultaneously collects sensitive user data. Additional permissions requested by the malicious app include access to contacts, call logs, SMS, account information, as well as an overview of all installed apps.</p><p>The website hosting the malicious file was created on October 12, 2023 and has since been taken offline. Only users who installed the Android version of the app from this specific website are impacted and urgently advised to delete the app. Users can determine if they installed the malicious version by reviewing the permissions granted to the RedAlert app. If users are unsure whether they installed the malicious version, they can delete the RedAlert applications and reinstall the legitimate version directly in the Play Store.</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/6nCyNtOTncD702msYn7mzW/9550d6742b8bbf6ba382d36166da4357/pasted-image-0--13-.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1600" height="1334" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>Screenshot of the attacker site </i><a href="https://redalerts\[.\]me"><i>https://redalerts\[.\]me</i></a></p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h3 id="malicious-android-package-kit-apk-analysis">Malicious Android Package Kit (APK) Analysis</h3>
            <a href="#malicious-android-package-kit-apk-analysis" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
        <p>The malicious Android Package Kit (APK) file is installed by a user when they click the Google Play button on the fake RedAlert site. Once clicked, the user downloads the app directly from the fake site at <code><i>hxxps://redalerts[.]me/app.apk</i></code>. The SHA-256 hash of the APK is <code><i>5087a896360f5d99fbf4eb859c824d19eb6fa358387bf6c2c5e836f7927921c5</i></code>.</p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h2 id="capabilities">Capabilities</h2>
            <a href="#capabilities" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
          <p>A quick analysis of the <i>AndroidManifest.xml</i> file shows several differences compared to the legitimate, open source RedAlert application. Most notable are the additional permissions needed to collect information on the victim. The permissions added are listed below:</p><ul><li><p>android.permission.GET_ACCOUNTS</p></li><li><p>android.permission.QUERY_ALL_PACKAGES</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_CALL_LOG</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_CONTACTS</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_PHONE_NUMBERS</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_PHONE_STATE</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_PRIVILEGED_PHONE_STATE</p></li><li><p>android.permission.READ_SMS</p></li></ul><p>The application is designed to look and act like RedAlert. However, upon opening the app, a malicious service is started in the background. The <code><i>startService()</i></code> call is the only change to the <code><i>onCreate()</i></code> method, and this begins the sequence of malicious activity, which the actor has placed in a package called <code><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI</i></code></p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5SOvfo0vzlyyREVB4A9Jyt/a3a971fe5b0860bb403528579a5f5393/pasted-image-0--14-.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1530" height="442" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>The attacker starts their malicious code within the legitimate RedAlert code com.red.alert.activities: Main.java</i></p><p>The service is run to gather data from victimsâ phones and upload it to the actorâs secure server. The data is extensive and includes:</p><ul><li><p>SIM information, including IMEI and IMSI numbers, network type, country, voicemail number, PIN status, and more</p></li><li><p>Full Contact list</p></li><li><p>All SMS messages, including content and metadata for all statuses (e.g. received, outgoing, sent, etc.)</p></li><li><p>A list of accounts associated with the device</p></li><li><p>All phone calls and conversation details for including incoming, outgoing, missed, rejected, and blocked calls</p></li><li><p>Logged-in email and app accounts</p></li><li><p>List of installed applications</p></li></ul><p>The actorâs code for gathering this information is illustrated below.</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/33VyzytviTDeG7qXy6aCrK/3f74918c7ceaaae9a9ce18fd650050a2/Screenshot-2023-10-13-at-3.32.27-PM.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1842" height="1326" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI: AIMain.java contains the data the attacker will capture form the target</i></p><p>Stolen data is uploaded to an HTTP server at a hardcoded IP address. The actor has a <i>Tools</i> class which details the IP address where the data is to be uploaded:</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5Fh4WgPsM5kmKnuM8Jqyxh/1307c4a8306bafcdfd47cc2f5e5323b8/Screenshot-2023-10-13-at-3.31.42-PM.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1896" height="668" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><b>com.company.allinclusive.AI: Tools.java stores the attackers command and control for the malware</b></p><p>Although HTTP and port 80 are specified, the actor appears to have the ability to use HTTPS and port 443 if a certificate is found bundled within the application package:</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/4ty1JMARyIggOGXmFoJjcE/7c4fe21747005a3882da8d2ca448583d/Screenshot-2023-10-13-at-3.30.20-PM.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="2000" height="935" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI: UploadFileAsync.java</i></p><p>Data is uploaded through a <i>Connector</i> class, written by the actor. The <i>Connector</i> is responsible for encrypting the stolen data and uploading it to the HTTP server. In this sample, files are encrypted with AES in CBC mode with PKCS5 Padding. The keys are randomly generated and appended to the packaged data, however the keys are encrypted with RSA using a public key bundled in the malicious app. Because of this, anybody who is able to intercept the stolen data will be unable to decrypt it without the actorâs private key.</p><p>The encrypted files have names that look like <i>_</i><i>.final</i>, which contain:</p><ul><li><p><i><b>_</b></i><i><b>.enc</b></i><b> (encrypted data)</b></p></li><li><p><i><b>_</b></i><i><b>.param</b></i><b> (AES encryption parameters, e.g. key and IV)</b></p></li><li><p><i><b>_</b></i><i><b>.eparam</b></i><b> (RSA parameters, e.g. public key)</b></p></li></ul>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h2 id="anti-analysis-runtime-capabilities">Anti-Analysis Runtime Capabilities</h2>
            <a href="#anti-analysis-runtime-capabilities" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
          <p>To avoid detection the actor included anti-analysis capabilities which can run at the time the app is started. The methods for anti-analysis that the attacker has included were anti-debugging, anti-emulation, and anti-test operations</p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h3 id="anti-debugging">Anti-Debugging</h3>
            <a href="#anti-debugging" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
        <p>The application makes a simple call using the builtin <i>android.os.Debug</i> package to see if the application is being debugged.</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7n1Dsyz3tBVwTCQDzQjCpu/62e2fcf823fee0b7c1f144d1d302c557/Screenshot-2023-10-13-at-3.29.28-PM.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1970" height="474" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI.anti.debugger: FindDebugger.java</i></p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h3 id="anti-emulation">Anti-Emulation</h3>
            <a href="#anti-emulation" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
        <p>The application attempts to locate certain files and identifiers to determine whether it is being run in an emulated environment. A snippet of these indicators are shown below:</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5oRGahgfmW0fqsFZ3L7Bi1/c63b68f780e19a3a3d8f005db7e15c50/pasted-image-0--12--1.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="1558" height="436" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI.anti.emulator: FindEmulator.java checks for common emulators</i></p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h3 id="anti-test">Anti-Test</h3>
            <a href="#anti-test" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
        <p>The application has utilities to identify whether a test user (âmonkeyâ) is using the application:</p>
            <figure class="kg-card kg-image-card ">
            
            <Image src="https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5bibuD77OAXj6pBVkBb012/9d5c06d0c17b43978e70bfe6101ea8d4/Screenshot-2023-10-13-at-3.28.48-PM.png" alt="" class="kg-image" width="2000" height="774" loading="lazy"/>
            
            </figure><p><i>com.company.allinclusive.AI.anti.monkey: FindMonkey.java</i></p><p>These methodologies are all rudimentary checks for whether the application is under runtime analysis. It does not, however, protect the malicious code against static analysis.</p>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h2 id="how-to-detect-this-malware-on-your-device">How To Detect This Malware On Your Device</h2>
            <a href="#how-to-detect-this-malware-on-your-device" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
          <p>If you have installed RedAlert on your device, the extraneous permissions added by the actor can be used to determine whether you have been compromised. The following permissions appearing on the RedAlert app (whether or not enabled) would indicate compromise:</p><ul><li><p>Call Logs</p></li><li><p>Contacts</p></li><li><p>Phone</p></li><li><p>SMS</p></li></ul>
          <div class="flex anchor relative">
            <h2 id="how-to-protect-yourself">How To Protect Yourself</h2>
            <a href="#how-to-protect-yourself" aria-hidden="true" class="relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5">
              <svg width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentcolor" d="m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z"></path></svg>
            </a>
          </div>
          <p>You can avoid attacks like this by following the guidance below:</p><ul><li><p>Keep your mobile device up to date on the latest software version at all times</p></li><li><p>Consider using Cloudflare Teams (with <a href="https://www.cloudflare.com/zero-trust/products/gateway/">Cloudflare Gateway</a>)</p></li><li><p>Avoid using third party mobile application stores</p></li><li><p>Never install applications from Internet URLs or sideload payloads</p></li><li><p>Consider using <a href="https://1.1.1.1/family/">1.1.1.1 for families</a> to block malicious domains on your network</p></li></ul>
